  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_326435c1-9587-4c6b-a688-c6d0fcf0bdc3'

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer15977696912210270908">When a murdered woman is found in the city of Beszel, somewhere at the edge of Europe, it looks to be a routine case for Inspector Tyador Borl√∫ of the Extreme Crime Squad. But as he investigates, the evidence points to conspiracies far stranger and more deadly than anything he could have imagined.<br/><br/>Borl√∫ must travel from the decaying Beszel to the only metropolis on Earth as</span>
  <span id="freeText15977696912210270908" style="display:none">When a murdered woman is found in the city of Beszel, somewhere at the edge of Europe, it looks to be a routine case for Inspector Tyador Borl√∫ of the Extreme Crime Squad. But as he investigates, the evidence points to conspiracies far stranger and more deadly than anything he could have imagined.<br /><br />Borl√∫ must travel from the decaying Beszel to the only metropolis on Earth as strange as his own. This is a border crossing like no other, a journey as psychic as it is physical, a shift in perception, a seeing of the unseen. His destination is Beszel‚Äôs equal, rival, and intimate neighbor, the rich and vibrant city of Ul Qoma. With Ul Qoman detective Qussim Dhatt, and struggling with his own transition, Borl√∫ is enmeshed in a sordid underworld of rabid nationalists intent on destroying their neighboring city, and unificationists who dream of dissolving the two into one. As the detectives uncover the dead woman‚Äôs secrets, they begin to suspect a truth that could cost them and those they care about more than their lives. <br /><br />What stands against them are murderous powers in Beszel and in Ul Qoma: and, most terrifying of all, that which lies between these two cities.<br /><br />Casting shades of Kafka and Philip K. Dick, Raymond Chandler and 1984, <i>The City &amp; the City</i> is a murder mystery taken to dazzling metaphysical and artistic heights.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15977696912210270908" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">E-book fonts seem to lack the accented z used in Beszel; the e-book version I read (on Kobo) had the accented z as effectively an image, so it was in <a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">‚Ä¶more</a></span><span class="fullContent">E-book fonts seem to lack the accented z used in Beszel; the e-book version I read (on Kobo) had the accented z as effectively an image, so it was in a different font to the rest of the text.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/222087170?book_show_action=true">Oct 11, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bill Kerwin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bill Kerwin" href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin">Bill Kerwin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=detective-mystery">detective-mystery</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer222087170" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15742146777957577392"><br/>The premise is extraordinarily interesting and meticulously developed. The question posed: what if two opposed cities existed side by side (with more than an occasional overlap) but were separated, not by an actual wall like East and West Berlin, but by the deeply inculturated habit of deliberate ignorance, a studied denial of the other, a fierce determination not to see? The central dilemma: when a murder is committed in one city, and the body is dumped in the other, how do the detectives inves</span>
  <span id="freeText15742146777957577392" style="display:none"><br />The premise is extraordinarily interesting and meticulously developed. The question posed: what if two opposed cities existed side by side (with more than an occasional overlap) but were separated, not by an actual wall like East and West Berlin, but by the deeply inculturated habit of deliberate ignorance, a studied denial of the other, a fierce determination not to see? The central dilemma: when a murder is committed in one city, and the body is dumped in the other, how do the detectives investigate the crime without violating the taboos of their society?<br /><br />The first third of the book (and a brief coda at the end) are filled with symbolic resonance. Unfortunately, however, the mystery itself is conventional, the action predictable, and the murderer easily guessed. The second third is less effective than the first, and the final section less effective still.<br /><br />If Mieville had embodied the symbolic essence of his original conceit in working out his plot, this might have been a work worthy of Kafka and Schulz--its major influences. As is, it is a so-so mystery with a hauntingly memorable setting.<br /><br />Then again . . . the setting continues to resonate long after the book is completed, for it has much to say to us about the uneasy compromises we make in a complex urban environment. Perhaps, after all, this resonance is enough.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15742146777957577392" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2889171595" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2889171595" />
    <a title="Glenn Russell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23385697-glenn-russell"><img alt="Glenn Russell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505195595p2/23385697.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2889171595?book_show_action=true">Jul 12, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Glenn Russell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Glenn Russell" href="/user/show/23385697-glenn-russell">Glenn Russell</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23385697-glenn-russell?shelf=favorite-books">favorite-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2889171595" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5806445577313828043"><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1562706148i/27800253._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Wow! Make that Double Wow!<br/><br/>Unsurpassed imagination and invention. All within the context of detective novel that's a turbocharged page-turner. <br/><br/>The cityscape China Mi√©ville creates here is neither Kafka absurdist nor Dali surreal, his novel‚Äôs two cities are every bit as substantial and realistic as Jo Nesb√∏‚Äôs Oslo or Tana French's Dublin or Stieg Larsson‚Äôs Stockholm ‚Äì and it‚Äôs this grounding in realism that makes Mi√©ville's weird elements all the weirder.<br/><br/>I completely agree with Stephen King who ha</span>
  <span id="freeText5806445577313828043" style="display:none"><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1562706148i/27800253._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Wow! Make that Double Wow!<br><br>Unsurpassed imagination and invention. All within the context of detective novel that's a turbocharged page-turner. <br><br>The cityscape China Mi√©ville creates here is neither Kafka absurdist nor Dali surreal, his novel‚Äôs two cities are every bit as substantial and realistic as Jo Nesb√∏‚Äôs Oslo or Tana French's Dublin or Stieg Larsson‚Äôs Stockholm ‚Äì and it‚Äôs this grounding in realism that makes Mi√©ville's weird elements all the weirder.<br><br>I completely agree with Stephen King who has stated emphatically again and again that book reviewers and writers of dust jackets usually go overboard and give away far too much, especially when it comes to mysteries and thrillers. Thus I'll avoid plot development and stick with describing a number of keys for <i>The City &amp; The City</i>. <br><br>ONE BODY <br>Crime scene - In the weeds in an open area among drab buildings, police gather around a murder victim, a twenty-something woman, naked. Was she a working girl? A hooker? Why was she dumped here, far out from the heart of their city of Be≈∫el? The name of the first detective on the scene - Bardo Naustin. Nice touch, China! Bardo is a term from Tibetan Buddhism referring to ‚Äúthe between‚Äù usually associated with the state between an individual‚Äôs final breath here on earth and their next rebirth. Much of the novel‚Äôs action takes place in those multidimensional ‚Äúbetween‚Äù spaces, between the two cities, between myth and history, between perception and reality. <br><br>TWO CITIES<br>As if out on the fringes of Eastern Europe, poor, dingy Glasgow and rich, glittering Dubai are contiguous, so close the cities share streets and intersections as well as intersecting histories. For China Mi√©ville, these two cities are Bes≈∫el and Ul Qoma. <br><br>Bes≈∫el ‚Äì ‚ÄúPoverty deshaped the already staid, drab cuts and colours that enduringly characterized Bes≈∫ clothes ‚Äì what had been called the city‚Äôs fashionless fashion.‚Äù Over the course of two hundred years, refugees from the Balkans flooded into the city‚Äôs previously Jewish ghettos, swelling the number of Muslims. For many decades it has been common to see coffeehouses side by side, one Jewish, one Muslim, with a single name and sharing tables and chairs for customers. Also worth noting, the few booming Bes≈∫el businesses include liquor stores and women selling sex. However, with its unique geography and architecture, including many cathedrals, Bes≈∫el still attracts a fair share of tourists. <br><br>Ul Qoma ‚Äì Money and more money from foreign investors ‚Äì mostly Canadian ‚Äì has been flowing into this secular, slick, ultramodern metropolis. Tall skyscrapers are rapidly replacing traditional baroque buildings and one prime Ul Qoma attraction is the impressive Temple of Inevitable Light. There are sports stadiums and community gardens and parks where Ul Qoman natives mingle with Kurds, Pakistanis, Somalis and Sierra Leoneans. Ul Qoma has its own language and dialects, mostly but not entirely separate from the language of Bes≈∫el. <br><br>THREE DETECTIVES <br>Inspector Tyador Borl√∫ of the Bes≈∫ian Extreme Crime Squad - Our perceptive first person narrator is a native of Bes≈∫el and is totally dedicated to uncovering the truth surrounding the murder of that twenty-something young lady. Inspector Borl√∫‚Äôs personality and dogged relentlessness call to mind investigative journalist Mikael Blomkvist from Steig Larsson‚Äôs <i>Millennium</i> series. <br><br>Constable Lizbyet Corwi - Officially, she's Borl√∫'s assistant but her intelligence and abilities with the computer make her more of a partner than mere assistant. Very much like Borl√∫, Lizbyet Corwi is a tenacious truth seeker willing to make sacrifices and put her life in danger in order to crack a case. <br><br>Senior Detective Qussim Dhatt - The murder investigation takes Inspector Borl√∫ to Ul Qoma. He is assigned to work with Dhatt, a hardliner from the old school. Dhatt detests what he considers ragtag troublemakers, such as the unificationists who want nothing more than to obliterate any distinction between the two cities. Dhatt hates to work outside official lines so when Borl√∫ brings certain facts to his attention, the Ul Qoma senior detective has a series of life and death decisions to make. <br><br>FOUR BITS OF WEIRDNESS <br>"Unseeing" - A truly unique part of the novel. The citizens of Bes≈∫el are trained at an early age to "unsee" anything relating to Ul Qoma, the people, the buildings, their merchandise, their streets. If someone is caught not unseeing there will be most unpleasant consequences. Likewise, for citizens of Ul Qoma - they are required to "unsee" anything relating to Bes≈∫el. This choosing to see and not see reminds me of the 18th century philosopher George Berkeley and his "to be is to be perceived." In other words, if I do not see something, that something does not exist. <br><br>Crosshatching - Areas and street and intersections belonging to both Bes≈∫el and Ul Qoma. All citizens from both cities must be particularly alert when approaching or crossing such crosshatched areas since one wouldn't want to be caught not unseeing. This even goes for the police - many the time Borl√∫, Corwi and Dhatt flip into a kind of hyperawareness when dealing with crosshatching. <br><br>Breach - The truly weird part of <i>The City and The City</i> - Breach is a shadowy third city existing in the hidden margins between Bes≈∫el and Ul Qoma. Their police force seems to have powers bordering on the miraculous. If someone violates their laws and "breaches" then those unfortunates are instantly swarmed on by the powers of Breach. And perhaps never heard from again. And Breach doesn't abide by either city's laws; rather, they establish and enforce their own laws. The forces of Breach - not only weird but eerie and creepy bordering on sinister. <br><br>Orciny - There are legends and myths and fairy tales revolving around the ancient city of Orciny that predates Bes≈∫el and Ul Qoma. Some say the ancient city still exists in secret hidden places. Since ultimate power is very much part of the legend of Orciny, there are speculations as to its relationship with Breach. Is Orciny the ultimate counterforce to Breach? Or, perhaps is Orciny just another name for Breach? Mystery upon mystery. <br><br>FIVE CONCLUDING OBSERVATIONS<br>Linguistics - For those readers interested in language, China Mi√©ville's novel will be a treat. Here's a snip of what the author offers: "If you do not know much about them, Illitan and Bes≈∫ sound very different. They are written, of course, in distinct alphabets. Bes≈∫ is in Bes≈∫: thirty-four letters, left to right, all sounds rendered clear and phonetic, consonants, vowels and demivowels decorated with diacritics - it looks, one often hears, like Cyrillic (though that is a comparison likely to annoy a citizen of Bes≈∫el, true or not). Illitan uses Roman script. This is recent."<br><br>High Culture and Popular Culture - The novel takes place in the first decade of the 21st century. The culture appears to be not that much different from what a traveler would find in Paris or London (two cities Tyador Borl√∫ has visited in his earlier years). There's mention of one underground novel about secret forces at work in the two cities and Borl√∫ reflects on a "glitzy Ul Qoman Fast Economy Zone full of horrible but big public art," but that's about all the references to literature and the arts.<br><br>Violent Crime Fiction - Readers wary of gratuitous violence will be pleased to know there is none to be found in this China Mi√©ville novel. Matter of fact, the violence in <i>The City &amp; The City</i> is nowhere even close to what one will encounter in Jo Nesb√∏ or Stieg Larsson. <br><br>Dogs and Cats? - I don't recall any dogs or cats but what I do recall is mention of wolves prowling around in both cities. One of the curiosities I wasn't expecting. <br><br>Genres - Is <i>The City &amp; The City</i> science fiction or fantasy? Well, perhaps there are elements of both at work in the novel but first and foremost this is a crime mystery, where the detectives chase down clues in both cities and all the weirdness in between. A special book - one very much worth the read.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1563044768i/27822952._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>British author China Mi√©ville, born 1972</span>
  <a data-text-id="5806445577313828043" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_693641644" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/693641644" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/693641644?book_show_action=true">Aug 13, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="BlackOxford" class="user" itemprop="url" name="BlackOxford" href="/user/show/17744555-blackoxford">BlackOxford</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=british">british</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=fantasy-horror">fantasy-horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer693641644" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9811794854591022567"><b>O Happy Fault</b><br/><br/>I have never underestimated China Mieville‚Äôs writing talent. But until recently I also hadn‚Äôt realized the depths of his thought. <i>The City & the City</i> is not merely a cleverly structured detective novel, it is also a rather profound anthropological analysis.<br/><br/>The premise of the book is that the City in question is divided in two by a sort of psychological Iron Curtain, sometimes at the level of individual dwellings. The two parts of the City intertwine physically, but the residents of </span>
  <span id="freeText9811794854591022567" style="display:none"><b>O Happy Fault</b><br /><br />I have never underestimated China Mieville‚Äôs writing talent. But until recently I also hadn‚Äôt realized the depths of his thought. <i>The City &amp; the City</i> is not merely a cleverly structured detective novel, it is also a rather profound anthropological analysis.<br /><br />The premise of the book is that the City in question is divided in two by a sort of psychological Iron Curtain, sometimes at the level of individual dwellings. The two parts of the City intertwine physically, but the residents of each half are not permitted to see, hear, smell, touch, or otherwise interact with the residents of the other half. Each population is restricted to its designated spaces in which everyone lives apparently normal lives but with no awareness of the others who live among them.<br /><br />Residents from each half may visit the other half by transferring through a sort of border tunnel in the middle of the City. Having crossed from one half to the other, the visitor is required to participate fully in its life. He or she must ‚Äòunsee‚Äô everything with which he is intimately familiar from his half. Any lapse in this protocol is considered a Breach and is dealt with harshly as a matter of law.<br /><br />There are certainly a variety of ways in which Mieville‚Äôs imagination can be interpreted: for example, as a representation of the human psychological ability to simply ignore what it does not wish to see; or as the regrettable compartmentalization of modern life in which certain moral behaviors are permissible in one ‚Äòbox‚Äô but anathema in another; or as a critique of the economic, social, and racial ghettoization of not just cities but also of whole societies.<br /><br />My first reading resulted in an interpretation of the City as wallowing in an unfortunate fate, implicitly waiting and hoping for some sort of redemptive unification of the City. The separation of the two parts of the City was the result of an obscure historical act equivalent to Original Sin. No one remembers what the act was or when it was committed but its effects persist in the rigid and unnecessary conventions that dominate the City‚Äôs life.<br /><br />Upon reflection, I have come to a very different interpretation of the book thanks to the influence of an unexpected source, a discussion of so-called ‚Äòreligious aesthetics‚Äô by a theologian named Frank Burch Brown [see: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2274323054">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a>]. Brown‚Äôs analysis opens up a very different possibility for understanding Mieville‚Äôs idea of parallel worlds occupying the same geographical space but alien to one another. <br /><br />This alternative explanation is based on some rather interesting observations on aesthetics, the study of choosing the filters by which we allow ourselves to perceive the world. Generalizing his conception a bit, what Brown suggests is that Mieville‚Äôs type of split world is not a flaw or distortion but a necessary condition for human beings to avoid falling into the trap of their own hubris. <br /><br />Each world in fact helps to make the other visible. The unique social conventions, mores, architecture, literature, cuisine, and routines of one can never be taken entirely for granted because there is another set of these cultural conditions, literally just around the corner. This fact ensures that neither half of the City can ever turn itself, its particular concerns, aims, and prejudices into idols because ‚ÄòThis is the way the world really is.‚Äô They are forced in daily life to recognize the existence of the ‚Äòother‚Äô precisely through the persistent demand to not notice it. Brilliant.<br /><br />So the ‚Äòsplit personality‚Äô of <i>The City &amp; the City</i> is not a flaw resulting from some horrible primordial mistake, but a well-conceived design executed by some wise folk to to keep the residents from that most dreadful mortal sin of believing one‚Äôs own press.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9811794854591022567" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_311110253" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/311110253" />
    <a title="Nataliya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya"><img alt="Nataliya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605995014p2/3672777.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/311110253?book_show_action=true">Apr 12, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nataliya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nataliya" href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya">Nataliya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780345497529" href="/book/show/6811283-the-city-the-city">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=hugo-nebula">hugo-nebula</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=strange-avenues-of-mieville-s-mind">strange-avenues-of-mieville-s-mind</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=2012-reads">2012-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=locus-winner">locus-winner</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=location-is-the-true-protagonist">location-is-the-true-protagonist</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer311110253" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5338312232172174427">Wow. <b>Okay, I'm definitely fangirling for China Mi√©ville.</b> I love his limitless imagination, the skill to effortlessly make an unbelievable premise feel real, and ability to turn any setting and place into a true protagonist.<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380374862i/729182._SX540_.jpg' width='400' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><u>SOME SPOILERS MAY HAVE CREEPED IN SOMEHOW, SO BE WARNED</u><br/><br/>This is my first non-Bas Lag novel, set in the (more or less) real world. But no reason to worry - <b>this remains as much of "weird fiction" as anything else by His Chinaness.</b> As Mi√©ville tries to write a novel in every genr</span>
  <span id="freeText5338312232172174427" style="display:none">Wow. <b>Okay, I'm definitely fangirling for China Mi√©ville.</b> I love his limitless imagination, the skill to effortlessly make an unbelievable premise feel real, and ability to turn any setting and place into a true protagonist.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380374862i/729182._SX540_.jpg" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><u>SOME SPOILERS MAY HAVE CREEPED IN SOMEHOW, SO BE WARNED</u><br><br>This is my first non-Bas Lag novel, set in the (more or less) real world. But no reason to worry - <b>this remains as much of "weird fiction" as anything else by His Chinaness.</b> As Mi√©ville tries to write a novel in every genre of fiction, this time he tackles <i>a <b>hardboiled noir crime mystery</b>.</i> A murder of a young woman, investigated by a slightly cynical but good and incorruptible detective Tyador Borl√∫ of Beszel Extreme Crime Squad, quickly evolves into a much larger mystery plot. It is a detective story, a hard-boiled crime with all the specifics and peculiarities of this genre. We have a murder mystery, high reliance on dialogue, fast-paced plot, many logical leaps and jumps that may confuse the reader about certain plot points but that still shed light on the story as a whole. <blockquote>"<i>We are all philosophers here where I am, and we debate among many other things the question of where it is that we live.</i>"</blockquote>But the mystery plot, albeit engaging and interesting, feels just as an excuse to introduce the reader to the <b>fascinating world of quasi-Eastern European twin cities of Besz√©l and Ul Qoma.</b> The cities are the true protagonists, not Detective Berl√∫ whose character is little more than an outline, the window into this world. Once a single city, Besz√©l and Ul Qoma were split apart by a mysterious Cleavage centuries ago. <blockquote>"<i>From that historically brief quite opaque moment, came the chaos of our material history, an anarchy of chronology, of mismatched remnants that delighted and horrified investigators.</i>"</blockquote><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380374862i/729183._SX540_.jpg" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>These are two separate nations with distinct languages, customs, clothes, economics. They do not like each other much. And yet they are not separated by any physical barrier - <b>the division between them is done by their citizens who have been conditioned since the early age to 'unsee' and 'unhear' the citizens of the other country,</b> even if they share the same streets and buildings in the 'crosshatched' areas belonging both to Besz√©l and Ul Qoma. <br><br>The cities share their past and present and their geography, but rigidly maintain the invisible lines of separation. Simply seeing and acknowledging someone from the other city - who can be within inches of you on the same street, on the same sidewalk, but yet in another country - is the ultimate crime, <b>the breach.</b><br> <br>And it is this semi-willing separation between Besz√©l and Ul Qoma that brings out the overarching themes of this book<b>. <i>The City &amp; The City</i> addresses the question of national identity and how it is determined.</b> There is much more than simple geography that goes into creating a people, a nation. There are subtler things like bits and pieces of learned behaviors, strange and puzzling to the foreigners beliefs and habits, time-tested social conventions, seemingly ridiculous taboos based on strange old traditions. <b>It's the amalgam of the little seemingly senseless and hard to understand things that defines a nation.</b> As I'm visiting my Eastern European motherland right now, I'm struck by the realization of the same - how much the national identity is the direct result of little idiosyncrasies<b>. <i>And the question arises - what will become of the nation itself if its beliefs and peculiarities are questioned? Is there a comeback from that</i>?</b><blockquote>"<i>It's not just us keeping them apart. It's everyone in Besz√©l and everyone in Ul Qoma. Every minute, every day. We're only the last ditch: it's everyone in the cities who does most of the work. It works because you don't blink. That's why unseeing and unsensing are so vital. No one can admit it doesn't work. So if you don't admit it, it does. But if you breach, even if it's not your fault, for more than the shortest time ... you can't come back from that.</i>"</blockquote>As usual, Mi√©ville presents us with superb and sophisticated world-building. The both cities are vivid and memorable, the atmosphere in both is depicted with skill and depth, and the nuances of this world are revealed subtly and unobtrusively without overt clunky exposition. As I came to expect from him, China Mi√©ville takes a concept that is rather difficult to swallow - the duality of this world, relying on little else but the tradition to keep it going - and develops it so well that by the end of the book it felt real to me.<br><br>The language of <i>The City &amp; The City</i>, when compared to the Bas Lag books, is quite simple, even minimalistic. It is not luxurious or flowing; on the contrary, it is crisp, clear, and devoid of any extraneous words, any extraneous descriptions, any possible fluff. It was the first book by Mi√©ville that I found a quick and easy read. And yet, despite the surface easiness, it is still incredibly sophisticated and very visual.<br><br><b>This book fully deserves 4.5 stars.</b> I highly recommend it both to Mi√©ville fans and those who for whatever strange reason have not read his books yet. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5338312232172174427" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2352164264" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2352164264" />
    <a title="J.L.   Sutton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3675162-j-l-sutton"><img alt="J.L.   Sutton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1438142892p2/3675162.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2352164264?book_show_action=true">Apr 05, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J.L.   Sutton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J.L.   Sutton" href="/user/show/3675162-j-l-sutton">J.L.   Sutton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2352164264" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9971749402099979609">What at first simply seems interesting (the overlapping geographical space of the two distinct cities of Beszel and Ul Qoma) by turns becomes an absorbing part of the mystery in China Mieville's The City & the City. As Extreme Crime Squad Inspector Borlu investigates the death of archaeology student, Mahalia Geary, it quickly becomes clear that so much more is going on than just a murder. Mieville's novel combines 'weird fiction' (the genre with which Mieville is associated) with the police proc</span>
  <span id="freeText9971749402099979609" style="display:none">What at first simply seems interesting (the overlapping geographical space of the two distinct cities of Beszel and Ul Qoma) by turns becomes an absorbing part of the mystery in China Mieville's The City &amp; the City. As Extreme Crime Squad Inspector Borlu investigates the death of archaeology student, Mahalia Geary, it quickly becomes clear that so much more is going on than just a murder. Mieville's novel combines 'weird fiction' (the genre with which Mieville is associated) with the police procedural (in the tone of noir detective fiction). It took me a bit of time to fully immerse myself in this world, but once I did I really enjoyed it! <br /> <br />The way Mieville delineates the shared geographical spaces of the two cities and the rules surrounding what constitutes Beszel and what Ul Qoma is really incredible. How the residents of the two cities must (upon penalty of Breach) treat the cities becomes inseparable from the plot (solving the murder). Admittedly, solving the case takes a backseat to the novel's other concerns. Still, it's interesting to see how residents of Beszel and Ul Qoma learn how to 'unsee' what isn't really part of their world. This is something I think happens in lots of places we live. What, for instance, constitutes the city we live in and that which we refuse to see or acknowledge in the same city? <br /><br />If residents of either Beszel or Ul Qoma want to visit the other city, they must cross over at a designated point (with proper paperwork). When Inspector Borlu tells a joke about the weather in the other city being worse, everyone knows it's a joke, but they can't treat it that way. I thought the moment was funny, but it was also revealing of the way the world is constructed. This world becomes more complicated when it's suggested that Orsini (a third city) might also share the same space as Beszel and Ul Qoma. <br /><br />This was my time reading China Mieville's work, but I plan to read more!</span>
  <a data-text-id="9971749402099979609" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_611615210" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/611615210" />
    <a title="carol. " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3978225-carol"><img alt="carol. " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307934801p2/3978225.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/611615210?book_show_action=true">May 09, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="carol. " class="user" itemprop="url" name="carol. " href="/user/show/3978225-carol">carol. </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=hugo">hugo</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=my-library">my-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=awards">awards</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=my-library-hardcover">my-library-hardcover</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer611615210" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10792803312173961980">Can a city have a personality? I think so. Certainly the feel of Los Angeles is entirely different from NYC, and different again from Chicago, right? But what are the components to a city's character? Despite being the centerpiece of the novel, <i>The City and the City</i> never came alive for me. Half the time I felt as if I was reading a dusty encyclopedia description of a city and half the time an oddly paced but elaborate mystery.<br/><br/>The story begins typical for the detective-mystery genre: we follow I</span>
  <span id="freeText10792803312173961980" style="display:none">Can a city have a personality? I think so. Certainly the feel of Los Angeles is entirely different from NYC, and different again from Chicago, right? But what are the components to a city's character? Despite being the centerpiece of the novel, <i>The City and the City</i> never came alive for me. Half the time I felt as if I was reading a dusty encyclopedia description of a city and half the time an oddly paced but elaborate mystery.<br /><br />The story begins typical for the detective-mystery genre: we follow Inspector Tyador Borlu of the Extreme Crime Squad to the scene of a murdered and nude woman. It is a seedy, run-down area full of addicts, and at first it appears as if she's another lost soul from the streets. Tyador commanders an eager young subordinate, Corwi, for her street contacts and legwork. Before long, an anonymous tip points them in the direction of Il Qoma, the city that lives within/alongside/interstices with their own city of Beszel. The citizens of both places are monitored by Breach, a mysterious authority who will spirit away those who mistakenly acknowledge the partner city without following proper channels.<br /><b><br />"'Why were you there?'<br />'... It was a conference. 'Policing Split Cities.' They had sessions on Budapest and Jerusalem and Berline, and Beszel and Ul Quoma.'<br />'Fuck!'<br />'I know, I know. That's what we said at the time. Totally missing the point.'<br />'Split cities? I'm surprised the acad let you go.'"<br /></b><br />The idea of two cities, co-existing in shared spaces but actually separate nations, is a brilliant idea. Unfortunately, Big Idea is all that holds this story together, which has little to humanize it amidst a slow and painful investigation. It occurred to me that this is the premise of so many other books, the unseen co-existing with the seen, but I felt it was hard to get a grip on the intricacies of the schism.<br /><br />Bezel and Il Quoma never truly differentiated for me. I think part of the reason is that Mieille relies on two main modes of describing his cities: the architecture and the political history. I felt very much like I was being given a lesson on Yugoslavia, or walled-Berlin, or one of the split cities so overtly mentioned. An example from page 43:<br /><b><br />"In typical political cliche, unificationists were split on many axes. Some groups were illegal, sister-organisations in both Beszel and Ul Qoma. The banned had at varioius points in their history advocated the use of violence to bring the cities to their God-, destiny-, history-, or people-intended unity. Some had, mostly cack-handedly, targeted nationalist intellectuals--bricks through windows and shit through doors. They had been accused of furtively propagandising among refugees and new immigrants with limited expertise at seeing and unseeing, at being in one particular city. The activists wanted to weaponise such urban uncertainty."<br /></b><br />I can appreciate such description, but does it resonate? Evoke emotion? I think of Kate Griffin's Matthew Swift series, how London comes alive with the lyrical descriptions, and wonder at the difference. Perhaps people would have helped, or further character-building of the ones we had. Histories of the characters could have been used to gain more insight into the schism between the two, but only Tyador's youth is shared in any detail. About the only person that stood out for me was the detective's superior, who humorously managed to undercut Borlu's complaints with every line of dialogue.<br /><b><br />"'This is bullshit. We've been screwed.'<br />'It is bullshit, he tells me,' Gadlem said to the world. 'He tells me we've been screwed.'<br />'We've been screwed, sir. We need Breach. How the hell are we supposed to do this? Someone somewhere is trying to freeze this where it stands.'<br />'We've been screwed, he tells me, and I note he tells me so as if I am disagreeing with him. Which when last I looked I was not doing.'"<br /></b><br />I enjoyed China's word-play, particularly 'grosstopically,' reference to things that are near in physical space but from different nations But the word-smithing didn't feel as sophisticated as <i>Embassytown</i> or as fun as <i>Kraken,</i> which is interesting, as they were published in a three-year span. Most of the vocabulary was created around the idea of 'unseeing' the neighbor city and it's occupants. Overall, the language felt stilted and excessively formal for genre fiction, further distancing me from the story.<br /><br />It's not that I don't understand the exploration of the dissonant, conjoined cities. We take the noir detective format, incorporate a nice play on the idea of two cities, merged but unseen, occupying almost the same space with each other. It's actually a relatively common exercise in the spec-fic/sci-fi world, giving an author (and reader) familiar concepts to latch onto while the author forays into stranger places. There are times the Big Idea works well and can carry a novel on its own, but for me, this wasn't it. I'd suggest <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/920395.Only_Forward" title="Only Forward by Michael Marshall Smith" rel="nofollow noopener">Only Forward</a> if you want to play with the idea of city and identity.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10792803312173961980" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText15477193588885568000" style="display:none">Full on ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ! This book is special. It's my first encounter with China Mieville and I'm very intrigued to read more. What a mind bending story. The 2 city concept is so challenging &amp; fantastic, not just a great idea but something very hard to really grasp in detail.<br /><br />The language is brilliant and fits perfectly in creating this noir detective story feel, the way the story is told, unfolding it layer by layer without ever being presented with a final truth and creating an atmosphere of strangeness and fascination!<br /><br />In other books I would have taken issue with the lack of explanations starting very soon in the book. But the way Mieville presents the mysteries and the what-the-hell-is-happening-here is so authentic. It's written from the perspective of the main character - and he is not explaining himself to the reader.<br /><br />It didn't take long for me to get hooked on the story and there wasn't a moment of slowness in it for me. In my edition there was an interview with the author at the end, which I found deepend my understanding of the author without revealing more about the mystery. I'm looking forward to read more of his books. Thanks for the wonderful ride!<br /><br />I just learned this book is being adapted to film. I really would like to see how that turns out! David Morrissey will be playing the inspector.</span>

<div id="review_61109130" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/61109130" />
    <a title="Stephen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1413439-stephen"><img alt="Stephen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1311616811p2/1413439.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/61109130?book_show_action=true">Jun 25, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephen" href="/user/show/1413439-stephen">Stephen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=award-nominee-nebula">award-nominee-nebula</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=signed-first-or-limited-edition">signed-first-or-limited-edition</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=award-nominee-british-sf">award-nominee-british-sf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=award-nominee-arthur-c-clarke">award-nominee-arthur-c-clarke</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=multiple-award-nominee">multiple-award-nominee</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=crime">crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=award-nominee-world-fantasy">award-nominee-world-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=6-star-books">6-star-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer61109130" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17574547174143018914"><b>AN AMAZING, EARTH-SHATTERING MAKE UP <s>SEXUAL</s> READING EXPERIENCE!!</b><br/><a href='http://s153.photobucket.com/albums/s207/sullypython/Hollywood%20moviess/?action=view&current=56a5229a-1-1.jpg' target='_blank' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380325126i/690995.jpg' alt='Photobucket' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><br/>6.0 stars. We all know that relationships have there ups and downs and that spats are going to happen even to the strongest of them. Well a few months ago, after having a couple of incredible years with China Mieville‚Äôs books, (i.e., <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/68494.Perdido_Street_Station' title='Perdido Street Station by China Mi√©ville' rel='nofollow noopener'>Perdido Street Station</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/68497.The_Scar__New_Crobuzon___2_' title='The Scar (New Crobuzon, #2) by China Mi√©ville' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Scar</a> and ), both of which are among my ALL TIME FAVORITES...suddenly turmoil. The cause of the turmoil was <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1861938.Un_Lun_Dun' title='Un Lun Dun by China Mi√©ville' rel='nofollow noopener'>Un Lun Dun</a>, which I just did not like and thought was <b>UGH-LA</b></span>
  <span id="freeText17574547174143018914" style="display:none"><b>AN AMAZING, EARTH-SHATTERING MAKE UP <s>SEXUAL</s> READING EXPERIENCE!!</b><br><a href="http://s153.photobucket.com/albums/s207/sullypython/Hollywood%20moviess/?action=view&amp;current=56a5229a-1-1.jpg" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380325126i/690995.jpg" alt="Photobucket" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>6.0 stars. We all know that relationships have there ups and downs and that spats are going to happen even to the strongest of them. Well a few months ago, after having a couple of incredible years with China Mieville‚Äôs books, (i.e., <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/68494.Perdido_Street_Station" title="Perdido Street Station by China Mi√©ville" rel="nofollow noopener">Perdido Street Station</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/68497.The_Scar__New_Crobuzon___2_" title="The Scar (New Crobuzon, #2) by China Mi√©ville" rel="nofollow noopener">The Scar</a> and ), both of which are among my ALL TIME FAVORITES...suddenly turmoil. The cause of the turmoil was <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1861938.Un_Lun_Dun" title="Un Lun Dun by China Mi√©ville" rel="nofollow noopener">Un Lun Dun</a>, which I just did not like and thought was <b>UGH-LAME-DUMB</b>. <br><br>All of sudden, what I thought was going to be a story-book, literary bromance was rife with doubts, questions and a whole lot of uncertainty. Well all that is past like gas my friends. I am here to tell you that <i>The City &amp; The City</i> is the best apology/make-up reading experience I have ever had and has recharged my bookish man-crush on CM until it is once again at full throb. <br><br>I mean WOW! WOW! WUBBZY! everyone, this story is pure liquid gold, highly literate and puts the PRO back in prose in mind-bombing fashion. <a href="http://s153.photobucket.com/albums/s207/sullypython/Gifs/?action=view&amp;current=gifbarney.gif" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380325126ra/690996.gif" alt="Photobucket" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br>What I once might have said with irony, I now say with conviction...<b>CHINA...MIEVILLE...IS...THE MAN!!</b> Okay, as I imagine my gush will continue throughout this written version of a standing ovation, let me at least thumbnail the plot for you as it is a corker. <br><br>The book is framed as a crime/mystery/detective thriller. The main character is Tyador Borlu, a detective in the Extreme Crime Squad (ECS) for the City of Beszel, who is investigating the brutal murder of a young female student. While never geographically placed, Beszel is a fictional Eastern European city that I was thinking was somewhere in the vicinity of Bulgaria/Romania/Hungary. With me so far...okay... <br><br>...<b>Now enter the completely original, BRAIN-CRAMP-INDUCING, OMGnormously brilliant central Mievillian concept...</b>(<u>NOTE</u><i>: If you have mushrooms, peyote or some other mind-expanding substance, you might want to take some now before continuing</i><br><br>....AHHHHHH that‚Äôs better...but now...WOAH...everything is so GREEEEEN....sorry about that...where was I...oh yeah the awesomeness.<br><br>Major parts of Beszel are geographically located and exist in the same physical space as major parts of another city known as Ul Qoma. The history of how this happened is only hinted at and mostly shrouded in mystery but the result is that these two cities each have 3 distinct areas. Areas 1 and 2 are those parts of the cities that are 100% in one city or another (basically, what we would call normal). These are referred to by residents of the cities as either Total (what a citizen calls the parts that are 100% in their own city) or Alter (what a citizen calls the parts that are 100% in the ‚Äúother‚Äù city). <br><br>Still with me...good because here is where it gets real tricky dicky. The third ‚Äúarea‚Äù type is referred to as ‚Äúcross-hatched.‚Äù As the name implies, these are areas where the cities overlap and places appear in both cities. As Wackyspeedia puts it, ‚ÄúThese might be streets, parks or squares where denizens of both cities walk alongside one another, <i>
  <u>albeit unseen</u>
</i>.‚Äù <br><br>Now let‚Äôs talk about the ‚Äúalbeit unseen‚Äù because that is the crux of the fantastical element of the story. People from both cities are indoctrinated from birth to ‚Äúunsee‚Äù people from the other city in those areas that are cross-hatched. To acknowledge or ‚Äúsee‚Äù someone from another city is the single worst taboo in both cities (more so even than murder) and is referred to as ‚Äúbreach.‚Äù I know, kinda hurts the head, but believe me when I say that CM does such an amazing job with the story that after the first 75 to 100 pages you are completely immersed in the world and it actually makes sense. <br><br>That‚Äôs enough plot background. From there you are on your own but CM has your back and you are in the most capable of hands. <br><br><b>MOST IMPRESSIVE ASPECTS OF THE NOVEL</b><br><br><u>First</u>: The ability for Mieville to make this complex, cerebrally challenging world come to life and resonate with the reader. Basically everything I said above. <br><br><u>Second</u>: As good as the concept was, the execution of this tremendously difficult idea is the <b>single greatest accomplishment</b> of this story and something that is almost never done successfully with this kind of difficult, central concept. The book stays in character THE ENTIRE TIME. There is never a moment when Mieville writes himself into a ‚Äúcross hatched‚Äù corner or loses control of his grand idea. How many times have you been intrigued by a novel because of a cool idea only to have it be ‚Äútoo big‚Äù for the author to handle. <br><br>Well CM is up to the task. Once you accept the two cities occupying the same space, he never makes you regret it. There are no ‚Äúgotcha‚Äù moments where the world-building fails or where the reader is left saying ‚Äúthat doesn‚Äôt work.‚Äù <br><br>Another author I like and respect, Michael Moorcock, put it this way: <blockquote>
  <i> On many levels this novel is a testament to [CM‚Äôs] admirable integrity. Keeping his grip firmly on an idea which would quickly slip from the hands of a less skilled writer, Mi√©ville again proves himself as intelligent as he is original.</i>
</blockquote> EXACTLY.<br><br><u>Third</u>: The rest of my list are all things we have heard many times before applied to China Mieville, but they are worth repeating. Number three is his prose. Lush, poetic, beautiful and as addicting as a caramel sundae with ‚Äúcrack‚Äù sprinkles and a large order of duck fat french fries with liquid meth/heroin dipping sauce. <br><br><u>Fourth</u>: Dialogue, dialogue, dialogue...CM is the deity of dialogue, the god of gab, and the <s>k</s>Cing of conversation. His character interactions always feel authentic and believable and yet are so slick and breezy that the words slide right off the page. <br><br><u>Five</u>: Plot/Pacing has been the one area where even my favorite CM stories sometimes fall a little short. NOT this time. The plot and pacing are just about perfect. The plot is complex, intricate and oh so engrossing and yet the pacing skates along at very good clip. I think a big reason for this stems from the story being only 300 pages long (whereas PSS and The Scar were both in the 700 page realm). 300 pages is the perfect length for a mystery story and China hits the bull right in eye on this one. <br><br>Well that‚Äôs about it, but I feel like I have to say at least something negative about the book so that it feels like an even-handed review. Okay, here are two ‚Äúnegatives‚Äù: (1) the cover art was pretty mediocre and (2) the book is still not as good as Perdido Street Station. There, satisfied. <br><br>Apart from that, I loved this book. I give it 6.0 Stars, have added it to my list of All Time Favorite novels and am very pleased that CM and I were able to get over our recent troubles. HIGHEST POSSIBLE RECOMMENDATION!! <br></span>
  <a data-text-id="17574547174143018914" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_1908700437" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1908700437" />
    <a title="Philip" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30863291-philip"><img alt="Philip" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1527983001p2/30863291.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1908700437?book_show_action=true">Feb 23, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Philip" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Philip" href="/user/show/30863291-philip">Philip</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30863291-philip?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30863291-philip?shelf=urban-fantasy">urban-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30863291-philip?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30863291-philip?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30863291-philip?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30863291-philip?shelf=hugo">hugo</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30863291-philip?shelf=author-man">author-man</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1908700437" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17040468069914576194">4.5ish stars. <br/><br/>This is a simple, classic noir detective story. Except it's anything but a simple, classic noir detective story. The story at the heart of the novel isn't really out of the ordinary. But the <i>way</i> it takes place is bonkers. <br/><br/>The setting of the book is in two separate cities existing within the same geographical ("grosstopical") area. How is that possible? It's honestly pretty hard to figure out at first. There's very little exposition; we're thrown into this world, having it explained</span>
  <span id="freeText17040468069914576194" style="display:none">4.5ish stars. <br /><br />This is a simple, classic noir detective story. Except it's anything but a simple, classic noir detective story. The story at the heart of the novel isn't really out of the ordinary. But the <i>way</i> it takes place is bonkers. <br /><br />The setting of the book is in two separate cities existing within the same geographical ("grosstopical") area. How is that possible? It's honestly pretty hard to figure out at first. There's very little exposition; we're thrown into this world, having it explained in bits and pieces through the eyes of our narrator. Inspector Tyador Borlu is from the Extreme Crime Squad in Beszel, one of the titular Cities and he's got a mystery on his hands...<br /><br />Mieville gives us an absolutely brilliant interpretation of segregation, cultural differences, political influence, governmental authority etc. in his creation of this fascinating world <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[which is really just a different take on our own bizarre world- there are plenty of examples of "grosstopical separation" throughout history... <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. Is the world ridiculously stupid or immediately believable? We're left with lots of questions to ponder and discuss which is just as much fun as reading.<br /><br />I didn't find the characters or the dialogue particularly fascinating but that doesn't even matter. The true main characters of the story are the Cities themselves and they shine.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17040468069914576194" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
