    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_396185571" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/396185571" />
    <a title="Cecily" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily"><img alt="Cecily" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480285828p2/1199525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/396185571?book_show_action=true">Aug 20, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cecily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cecily" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily">Cecily</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330534192" href="/book/show/9791714-the-city-the-city">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=scifi-future-speculative-fict">scifi-future-speculative-fict</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=miscellaneous-fiction">miscellaneous-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=dystopian">dystopian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=detective-mystery-crime">detective-mystery-crime</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer396185571" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9412109502262952490">"<i>There's a series of random and implausible crises that make no sense other than if you believe the most dramatic possible shit. And there's a dead girl.</i>"<br/>That quote from a character in the book, sums this up very well.<br/><br/>I enjoyed the concept, the wordplay, and the impossibility of categorisation: it's a detective story, with strong political themes, but it's set in a world that is not exactly dystopian or futuristic or fantastic - but it isn't quite realistic either.<br/><br/><b>The Cities</b><br/><br/>The title relates to </span>
  <span id="freeText9412109502262952490" style="display:none">"<i>There's a series of random and implausible crises that make no sense other than if you believe the most dramatic possible shit. And there's a dead girl.</i>"<br>That quote from a character in the book, sums this up very well.<br><br>I enjoyed the concept, the wordplay, and the impossibility of categorisation: it's a detective story, with strong political themes, but it's set in a world that is not exactly dystopian or futuristic or fantastic - but it isn't quite realistic either.<br><br><b>The Cities</b><br><br>The title relates to a divided city that operates as separate cities, but it's not like Berlin, Budapest, Belfast, Nicosia or Jerusalem because <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[the two cities (Bes≈∫el and Ul Qoma) occupy the same geographical space. Instead, the separation is psychological and sensorial: citizens of each learn to unsee, unhear and even unsmell anything from the other city. If they don't, they "breach", and thereby invoke the vague but terrifying wrath of Breach. <br><br>There is also the mythical, secret, taboo city of Orciny, which some believe is "between the city and the city". <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br><br>In such a setup, no one fully belongs to their own city because in some senses, it is not fully a city. For those who have to visit the other city, things are even stranger. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1523112644i/25340572._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>It is this brilliantly weird central premise that makes the book so good. If you don't know about it when you start reading it, the clues are gradually built up, but knowing it, as I did, didn't spoil my enjoyment.<br><br>Ultimately, the division is maintained by consent, like the Emperor's New Clothes: "It's not just us keeping them apart. It's everyone in Bes≈∫el and Ul Qoma... It works because you don't blink." <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[That's why unseeing and unsensing are so vital. No one can admit it doesn't work. So if you don't admit it, it does. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Mind you, there is very limited political freedom in either city (UQ is a one-party state, in Bes≈∫el, dissident groups are monitored, and both cities are under the mysterious power of Breach), so the idea of consent is somewhat moot. <br><br><b>Differences Between the Cities</b> <br><br>I didn't get hung up on which real world cities might have inspired this (I doubt there would be a simple answer). However, I was interested in the ways in which they apparently differed, the "intense learning of cues" required of all children (and the few tourists). "We pick up on styles of clothing, permissible colours, ways of walking and holding oneself." Some colours are actually illegal in one city, and one is more diverse <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[(UQ has more Asians, Africans and Arabs, and it has spicier food, whereas Bes≈∫el has a more potato-based diet) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br><br>As a reader, one has to learn these cues very gradually. Even half way through I didn't have a very clear picture of the different appearance, culture or politics, other than that <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[UQ was somewhat richer, more technologically advanced and with better archaeological sites <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br><br>Their languages use different alphabets and it is heretical to say they are the same, and yet they are mutually intelligible.<br><br>Tyador Borl√∫, the hero and from whose point of view the story is told, is from Bes≈∫el, but I would rather live in UQ.<br><br><b>Murder Mystery and Themes</b><br><br>This situation creates a variety of intriguing and sometimes amusing complications and paradoxes which hamper police operations. The impetus of the story is the discovery and subsequent investigation of a woman's body, and uncertainty about which domain the crime occurred in, compounded by the fact she's not a resident of either city, but is American. There are disputed zones - shades of Rumsfeld's "known unknowns" and even when authority is agreed, the normal difficulties of solving a crime are compounded by the complexity of the two cities. <br><br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[It's difficult to get witness statements from people who are used to unseeing people and things, and who are ever fearful of accidentally seeing what they should not. There are even "Places that no one can see because they think they're in the other city". Chasing criminals without breaching is comical, but crucial. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> "Smuggling itself is not breach, though most breach is committed in order to smuggle."<br><br>These issues raise all sorts of questions about the nature and power of the state and its police, and particularly about the relevance of intent in determining whether something is a crime. "Because you may not see the justice of what we do doesn't mean it's unjust" (neither does it mean it <b>is</b> just).<br><br><b>Cop Drama Tropes</b><br><br>I'm not really a follower of detective stories, either on the page or on screen, but Mieville tips a hat to many of the clich√©s of the genre: good by psychologically scarred cop (Borl√∫)/bad cop (various, fluctuating, minor), the sparky relationship between partners (Borl√∫ with Corwi and later with Dhatt), following hunches, breaking the rules for the greater good, messy love life, a few car chases and so on. <br><br>The chapters are mostly short and punchy, and each ends with a revelation or cliff-hanger (or both). Yet it doesn't feel hackneyed, perhaps because the setting is so startlingly original. In fact, Mieville confronts the risk of clich√© head-on, writing of one character: <br>"<i>His fidelity to the clich√© transcended the necessity to communicate.</i>"<br><br><b>Wordplay and Writing Style</b><br><br>Mieville has fun with neologisms and a few existing but esoteric words. At times he explicitly defines them when context and etymology make that unnecessary (e.g. gudcop and mectec), which is irksome, but nevertheless, some of the words are good. For example:<br>* Grosstopically: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Two locations, each in a different city, but occupying the same geographical space in other terms. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br>* Topolganger: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[When grosstopical places look alike. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br>* Alterity: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Alternative, a grosstopically equivalent place, "A Bes≈∫ dweller cannot walk a few paces next door into an alter house without breach". <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br>* Insiles: Sort of the opposite of exiles.<br>* Glasnostroika: Glasnost + Perestroika, and the cities have echoes of central and eastern Europe.<br>* Gallimaufrians are mentioned: perhaps a nod to Dr Who?<br>* Cleavage: The reason for there being two cities - in <b>both</b> senses of the word: "was it schism or conjoining"... "split or convergence"?<br>* Crosshatching: A whole new meaning to a familiar word.<br><br>As in The Scar, there are a few awkward or ugly sentences that I had to reread, but far fewer. A couple of examples (for my own reference more than anything else):<br>"He came to UQ, from where he went to B, managed I do not know how to go between the two of them - legally I assure you - several times, and he claimed..." Just adding a single comma would make all the difference.<br>"Unlike for my distance viewing of the night, up close the walls blocked off the site from watchers."<br><br>There are others that are convoluted in a clever and amusing way, though: "I couldn't help fail to completely unsee"!<br><br>I think my only quibble with the story-telling is the quantity of rushed explanation and exposition towards the end, rather as Goldfinger or another James Bond baddie would do.<br><br><b>Favourite Quotes</b><br><br>* A dead body: "skin smooth that cold morning, unbroken by gooseflesh... like someone playing at dead insect, her limbs crooked, rocking on her spine... Her face was set in a startled strain. She was endlessly surprised by herself."<br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[* "Architecture broken by alterity... The local buildings are taller... so Bes≈∫el juts up semi regularly and the roof-scape is almost a machicolation... laced by the shadows of girded towers that would loom over it if they were there." <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br>* "Those most dedicated to the perforation of the boundary... had to observe it most carefully."<br>* At an archaeological site, "Security guards, keeping safe these forgotten then remembered memories".<br>* "the explosive percussion of the bullet into the wall... Architecture sprayed."<br>* There is an unreal, almost supernatural quality of Breach (and their forces have a distinctive and intimidating gait): "The soundlessness was enervating... he was a cutout of darkness, a lack... clothes as vague as my own... Their faces were without anything approaching expressions. They looked like people-shaped clay in the moments before God breathed out." And yet it turns out that Breach uses cameras to watch the fringes (shades of Peake's "Titus Alone"), when I was expecting something less tangible.<br>* "Students might stand, scandalously, touching distance from a foreign power, a pornography of separation."<br>* A helicopter is "percussion in the otherwise empty locked-down sky".<br>* "Schroedinger's pedestrian... That gait... rootless and untethered, purposeful and without a country... He.. strode with pathological neutrality."<br><br><b>Missed a Trick</b><br><br>The book mentions fracturedcity.org - twice - but it just redirects to the publisher's page: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.randomhouse.com/">http://www.randomhouse.com/</a>!<br><br><b>Related Reading</b><br><br>Mieville is the sort of author I expect and want to like, but I didn't feel the love with my first encounter, <b>The Scar</b> (see my review <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/72921519" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>). This second foray into his works was far more rewarding, and my third, <b>Embassytown</b>, was even more so (there are some interesting parallels, too, which I've outlined in my review <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/396183492" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>.<br><br>I read this in the middle of reading Alistair Reynolds' <b>Century Rain</b> (see my review <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/498532574" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>). Neither is typical of the author's works, but both are noirish detective thrillers, featuring archaeologists and set in two versions of a city, albeit a very different sort of separation. Reading one enhances enjoyment of the other.<br><br>An interesting Q&amp;A with China, here on GR: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/566606-ask-china-mi-ville">http://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/5...</a>, including references to TC&amp;TC.<br><br><b>BBC TV adaptation of 2018</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1523112644i/25340571._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I've watched the four-part series. I'm relieved and impressed. <br><br>From the start, there's much more explanation of the cities' unique setup, but that's probably necessary for the change of medium. <br><br>They've also changed Borl√∫'s love life, so that the stereotypically troubled cop has a specific and very personal interest in this particular murder investigation. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[His wife, who was interested in Orciny via the same professor the dead Mahalia worked with, is missing and maybe dead. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br>Generally, it's excellent. The look of it, especially. It was filmed in Manchester and Liverpool and somehow captures the slippery and more exotic culture and geography: lots of familiar things, but too much of a mix to pin it to a single place. Even the unseeing works.<br><br>Also, they seem to have set it up to make it easy to extend it to a series!<br><br>* 40 second trailer: <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=h9Ds23M9-RE" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>.<br>* Bes≈∫el Tourist Orientation 1-minute video: <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=CdalaDaC6ZA" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>.<br>* imdb listing: <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt7205264/?ref_=nv_sr_1" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>.</["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>

<div id="review_49098265" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/49098265" />
    <a title="Brad" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1022982-brad"><img alt="Brad" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442484575p2/1022982.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/49098265?book_show_action=true">Mar 12, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brad" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brad" href="/user/show/1022982-brad">Brad</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=speculative">speculative</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=urban-fantasy">urban-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=weird">weird</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=sci-fantasy">sci-fantasy</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer49098265" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3162088106730625645">I see why so many people are underwhelmed by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4703581.The_City___The_City' title='The City & The City by China Mi√©ville' rel='nofollow noopener'>The City and The City</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/33918.China_Mi_ville' title='China Mi√©ville' rel='nofollow noopener'>China Mi√©ville</a>'s strange and wonderful homage to the mystery genre and his mother.<br/><br/>It is because while <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4703581.The_City___The_City' title='The City & The City by China Mi√©ville' rel='nofollow noopener'>The City and The City</a> is both of those things, it is also -- and more powerfully -- a love letter to his fans and an act of oeuvre snobbery of the first order.<br/><br/>What <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/33918.China_Mi_ville' title='China Mi√©ville' rel='nofollow noopener'>Mi√©ville</a> has done is to build a story upon his favourite themes, and to require that his audience is familiar with other occurrences of these themes in his work to fu</span>
  <span id="freeText3162088106730625645" style="display:none">I see why so many people are underwhelmed by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4703581.The_City___The_City" title="The City &amp; The City by China Mi√©ville" rel="nofollow noopener">The City and The City</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/33918.China_Mi_ville" title="China Mi√©ville" rel="nofollow noopener">China Mi√©ville</a>'s strange and wonderful homage to the mystery genre and his mother.<br /><br />It is because while <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4703581.The_City___The_City" title="The City &amp; The City by China Mi√©ville" rel="nofollow noopener">The City and The City</a> is both of those things, it is also -- and more powerfully -- a love letter to his fans and an act of oeuvre snobbery of the first order.<br /><br />What <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/33918.China_Mi_ville" title="China Mi√©ville" rel="nofollow noopener">Mi√©ville</a> has done is to build a story upon his favourite themes, and to require that his audience is familiar with other occurrences of these themes in his work to fully appreciate what he's done (perhaps inadvertently, but there it is). The <i>unseen</i> and the <i>uncity</i> occur throughout his work in varying forms, but they come together in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4703581.The_City___The_City" title="The City &amp; The City by China Mi√©ville" rel="nofollow noopener">The City and The City</a> with an intensity and concreteness that he has only flirted with before.<br /><br />Saul Garamond (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/68498.King_Rat" title="King Rat by China Mi√©ville" rel="nofollow noopener">King Rat</a>), Silas Fennec (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/68497.The_Scar__New_Crobuzon___2_" title="The Scar (New Crobuzon, #2) by China Mi√©ville" rel="nofollow noopener">The Scar</a>), Toro &amp; Spiral Jacobs (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/68495.Iron_Council__New_Crobuzon___3_" title="Iron Council (New Crobuzon, #3) by China Mi√©ville" rel="nofollow noopener">Iron Council</a>), The Weaver (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/68494.Perdido_Street_Station__New_Crobuzon___1_" title="Perdido Street Station (New Crobuzon, #1) by China Mi√©ville" rel="nofollow noopener">Perdido Street Station</a>), are all characters that move unseen. Each have their own reasons for moving unseen and their own methods for achieving it, but all of them move in and through the spaces that others cannot see or fail to see or choose not to see. And all of the motives and reasons for unseeing these characters culminate in the Beszel/Ul Qoma /Breach unseeing that Inspector Tyador Borlu of the Besz Extreme Crime Squad moves through in his search for the killer of a young American archaeologist.<br /><br />But the murder mystery, and even the potential conspiracies that swirl around the murder, are nowhere near as important as the way these two cities crosshatch and overlay and grosstopic, and the way people from the mundane to the Breach move through and around and in and outside all the permutations of these places in one place.<br /><br />And that concept of cities being more than what we are willing to see is the other piece of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/33918.China_Mi_ville" title="China Mi√©ville" rel="nofollow noopener">Mi√©ville</a>'s narcissitically intertextual puzzle. In The City &amp; The City it is two cities in the same space with a possible third city in the cracks. In <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/68496.Un_Lun_Dun" title="Un Lun Dun by China Mi√©ville" rel="nofollow noopener">Un-Lun-Dun</a> it is an ab-city for every city. And in Reports Of Certain Events In London, Varmin Way is a rogue street that hides and moves and won't let itself be found, and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/33918.China_Mi_ville" title="China Mi√©ville" rel="nofollow noopener">Mi√©ville</a> himself is the care taker of the files that speak of the streets existence.<br /><br />And even when <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/33918.China_Mi_ville" title="China Mi√©ville" rel="nofollow noopener">Mi√©ville</a>'s cities are behaving like cities should, their presence is so powerful, like Armada and New Crobuzon, that they are almost entities in their own right.<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4703581.The_City___The_City" title="The City &amp; The City by China Mi√©ville" rel="nofollow noopener">The City and The City</a> is the culmination of just over ten years of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/33918.China_Mi_ville" title="China Mi√©ville" rel="nofollow noopener">China Mi√©ville</a>'s already impressive career, but it won't receive the love it deserves, at least not for now. Once David Fincher or some other visionary director decides to put it on film, however, it may well become <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/33918.China_Mi_ville" title="China Mi√©ville" rel="nofollow noopener">Mi√©ville</a>'s most respected work. Too bad Orson Welles wasn't still alive. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4703581.The_City___The_City" title="The City &amp; The City by China Mi√©ville" rel="nofollow noopener">The City and The City</a> would be right up his <i>dissensi</i>.</span>

  <span id="freeText1043501477816953133" style="display:none">China Mieville is to modern fiction as The Clash was to popular music of their day: fresh, alive, vibrant, powerful, edgy, dangerous, misunderstood and by all accounts ‚Äì original. <br><br>The City and The City is about as original an idea, concept, theme as I can imagine‚Äì and all put together nicely into an ostensible murder mystery. While that is the tone and structure, to me the real story was the absurdist city on top of or within, or beside or related to the other city. Or whatever. <br><br>Don‚Äôt want to add any spoilers but a reader will be well into the book on their own before they figure it out, if they do. Working well within its own parameters, I also saw the value of the concept in terms of allegory, perhaps a message about how boundaries, affected and arbitrary, are absurd and our beliefs and reliance in them equally as outlandish. <br><br>After reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6931246.Kraken" title="Kraken by China Mi√©ville" rel="nofollow noopener">Kraken</a>, I placed the absurdist label on that writing, but not the Kafkaesque absurdity but rather the Monty Python kind. This one has the unmistakable influence of Kafka, but still with the charm and humor of Cleese, Palin and the rest of the troupe. <br><br>A very good read.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1554844562i/27340017.jpg" width="300" height="180" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>

It is somewhat reminiscent of a Jack Vance story.<br/><br/>A murdered woman is found in the city of Beszel, somewhere at the edge of Europe. It looks to be a routine case for Inspector Tyador Borl√∫ of the Extreme Crime Squad. But as he investigates, the evidence points to conspiracies far stranger and mor</span>
  <span id="freeText16018431459029722206" style="display:none">This Subterranean Press hardcover is numbered 227 of 500. and is signed by China Mi√©ville.<br /><br />The City &amp; the City by China Mi√©ville <br /><br />This novel is A blend of near-future science fiction and police procedural. It is somewhat reminiscent of a Jack Vance story.<br /><br />A murdered woman is found in the city of Beszel, somewhere at the edge of Europe. It looks to be a routine case for Inspector Tyador Borl√∫ of the Extreme Crime Squad. But as he investigates, the evidence points to conspiracies far stranger and more deadly than anything he could have imagined.<br />Borl√∫ must travel from the decaying Beszel to the only metropolis on Earth as strange as his own. This is a border crossing like no other, a journey as psychic as it is physical, a shift in perception, a seeing of the unseen. His destination is Beszel‚Äôs equal, rival, and intimate neighbor, the rich and vibrant city of Ul Qoma. With Ul Qoman detective Qussim Dhatt, and struggling with his own transition, Borl√∫ is enmeshed in a sordid underworld of rabid nationalists intent on destroying their neighboring city, and unificationists who dream of dissolving the two into one. As the detectives uncover the dead woman‚Äôs secrets, they begin to suspect a truth that could cost them and those they care about more than their lives.<br />What stands against them are murderous powers in Beszel and in Ul Qoma: and, most terrifying of all, that which lies between these two cities.<br /><br /> Mieville provides no overall exposition in this book, leaving it up to readers to piece together the strange co-existence of Beszel and Ul Qoma.<br /><br />Mi√©ville was born in Norwich and brought up in Willesden, a neighborhood in northwest London, and has lived in the city since early childhood. He grew up with his sister and his mother, a teacher; his parents separated soon after his birth, and he has said that he "never really knew" his father. He is an alumnus of the public school Oakham School, where he studied for two years. In 1990, when he was eighteen, he lived in Egypt teaching English for a year, where he developed an interest in Arab culture and Middle Eastern politics.<br /><br />Mi√©ville acquired a B.A. in social anthropology from Cambridge in 1994, and a Master's with distinction and PhD in International Relations from the London School of Economics, the latter in 2001. Mi√©ville has also held a Frank Knox fellowship at Harvard. A book version of his Ph.D thesis, titled ‚ÄúBetween Equal Rights: A Marxist Theory of International Law‚Äù, was published in the United Kingdom in 2005. <br /></span>

<div id="review_155747477" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/155747477" />
    <a title="Dan Schwent" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/870755-dan-schwent"><img alt="Dan Schwent" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593973948p2/870755.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/155747477?book_show_action=true">Mar 21, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dan Schwent" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dan Schwent" href="/user/show/870755-dan-schwent">Dan Schwent</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780345497529" href="/book/show/6811283-the-city-the-city">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/870755-dan-schwent?shelf=new-weird">new-weird</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/870755-dan-schwent?shelf=crime-and-mystery">crime-and-mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/870755-dan-schwent?shelf=2011">2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/870755-dan-schwent?shelf=the-survey-monkey-haul">the-survey-monkey-haul</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer155747477" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13740700451741452558">Tyador Borlu of Beszel's Extreme Crime Squad is assigned to the murder case of an unknown woman. To find her killer, Borlu must go to the neighboring city of Ul Qoma and team with Qussim Dhatt of the Murder Squad. Can the two detectives from different cultures figure out who the victim is and why she was killed?<br/><br/>Wow. The core premise of The City & The City requires some explaining but I think I'm up to the task. Remember those perceptual illusions you were so enamored with when you were a kid? Li</span>
  <span id="freeText13740700451741452558" style="display:none">Tyador Borlu of Beszel's Extreme Crime Squad is assigned to the murder case of an unknown woman. To find her killer, Borlu must go to the neighboring city of Ul Qoma and team with Qussim Dhatt of the Murder Squad. Can the two detectives from different cultures figure out who the victim is and why she was killed?<br><br>Wow. The core premise of The City &amp; The City requires some explaining but I think I'm up to the task. Remember those perceptual illusions you were so enamored with when you were a kid? Like the old woman/young girl:<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380347264i/701628.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Okay. Instead of images of an old woman and a young girl, picture instead two cities that overlap. People from one city are trained from birth to unsee/unsense people and buildings in the other city. Still with me? Now picture a murder mystery set in one of the intersections between the two cities. Yeah, it was one mindbender of a read.<br><br>While The City &amp; The City is firmly in the new weird genre, it's also a gritty crime story, which makes it substantially more accessible than many of Mieville's works. Tyador Borlu and Qussim Dhatt are the bickering cops that secretly grow to respect one another as they unravel a mystery than snakes back and forth between the two neighboring cities. Kind of like Jim Belushi and Arnold in Red Heat, except good.<br><br>The thing that makes The City &amp; The City work is that the cities are quite different from one another. While Ul Qoma and Beszel aren't as detailed as New Crobuzon, they are both distinct entities. While the idea of being in one city or the other is really odd, Mieville does a good job of explaining it and making it seem plausible. How often do you actually remember what homeless people look like, for example.<br><br>The concept of the Breach is also part of the glue that holds the story together. Part police force, part bogey man, the Breach enforce the unseeing/unsensing of the other city, keeping people from going back and forth between cities with impunity.<br><br>There's not a lot else I can say without ruining key plot points. If they were to make a movie of one of China Mieville's books, this would be the one. I kept seeing Ewan MacGregor and Jason Statham as the cops. It reminds me of Grant Morrison's The Invisibles at times, Neverwhere at others, and Jeff Vandermeer's Finch at still other times. I didn't like it quite as much as Finch but it was still spectacular. For fans of both detective fiction and the new weird, it's definitely a must-read. 4.5 out of 5.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13740700451741452558" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_149952926" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/149952926" />
    <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye"><img alt="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594424898p2/5022264.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/149952926?book_show_action=true">Feb 22, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye">Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=read-2010">read-2010</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews-5-stars">reviews-5-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews">reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=mieville">mieville</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer149952926" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9525343382234155965"><b>Urban Recall</b><br/><br/>I read this almost 12 months ago, which makes it difficult to recall and recount the tone of the writing. <br/>However, I would like to make some general comments about the novel.<br/><br/><b>An Abstract High Concept Novel</b> <br/><br/>In one sense, it is an abstract high concept novel. <br/>What does this mean? <br/>It's high concept in the sense that it takes a basic concept and explores it in detail. <br/>And it doesn't stray very far away from that concept. <br/>It's not "Snakes on a Plane". <br/>It's far more abstract than that.<br/><br/><b>The C</b></span>
  <span id="freeText9525343382234155965" style="display:none"><b>Urban Recall</b><br /><br />I read this almost 12 months ago, which makes it difficult to recall and recount the tone of the writing. <br />However, I would like to make some general comments about the novel.<br /><br /><b>An Abstract High Concept Novel</b> <br /><br />In one sense, it is an abstract high concept novel. <br />What does this mean? <br />It's high concept in the sense that it takes a basic concept and explores it in detail. <br />And it doesn't stray very far away from that concept. <br />It's not "Snakes on a Plane". <br />It's far more abstract than that.<br /><br /><b>The Concept</b> <br /><br />The concept is basically: what if two cities and cultures or civilisations co-existed in the one physical city? <br />I don't mean two distinct areas with a wall separating them like Berlin or Jerusalem. <br />I don't mean two sides of the same street being administered by different councils or governments. <br />One analogy for the City in the novel would be one city populated by some people and administered by one council during the day, then everybody moves out and it's a different people and council at night. <br />If that makes sense so far, then this City has one difference. <br />It isn't separated by day and night. <br />The two Cities within the City co-exist in time, but in the same geographical space (hence, the City and the City). <br />Everybody is everywhere at the same time, but they are separate. <br />They walk down the same streets, they drive on the same roads, but they are legally separate. <br />They aren't allowed to look at each other or acknowledge each other's existence. <br />Each City must act and behave as if the other City isn't there. <br />So this is the abstract high concept.<br /><br /><b>Mixing Genres</b> <br /><br />Mieville explores this fantasy concept within the crime thriller genre. <br />Some readers question whether this is the right vehicle for the concept. <br />They feel it makes the cardinal error of mixing genres. <br />But I think it makes sense.<br /><br /><b>The Law</b> <br /><br />The two different Cities are basically legal constructs. <br />They need the law to make the separation concrete. <br />The law sits at the interface between the two Cities and enforces their separation. <br />Any breach has to be detected and punished by the law. <br />Therefore, when a breach occurs in the novel, we find out the true nature of the Cities, the law and the justice system.<br /><br /><b>The Corruption</b> <br /><br />As usual, there is too much law and not enough justice. <br />As you would expect, these institutions end up being corrupt, not because they are institutions, but because they are institutions established and maintained by humans for human reasons. <br />The novel is really the story of these institutions and their corruption.<br /><br /><b>The Detective and the Crime</b> <br /><br />Like crime fiction, it is told through the eyes of a detective investigating a small crime, which ultimately leads to the detection of a big crime. <br />You could almost say it is the story of the Crime and the Crime (so I will). <br /><br /><b>The Tone</b><br /><br />Whether Mieville gets the tone right is a subjective issue. <br />We don't know a lot about the back story of the characters, we don't get a lot of physical descriptions. <br />We do get a well-constructed feel for the Kafkaesque atmosphere that defines this city. <br />People walk its streets like automatons or ghosts, unwilling to look left or right, for fear that they will breach the separation of the two Cities. <br />So if characters don't seem like they're human, if a reader feels that they are just cyphers, it's because the City has stripped their humanity away, it's the result of a stylistic choice (not the result of poor writing).<br /><br /><b>Abstract but Powerful</b> <br /><br />I admired Mieville's ability to construct these worlds and explore them credibly, while making the novel work at a metaphorical and political level. <br />The novel could describe life in a totalitarian state, it could describe life in a separated city or nation, but it might also symbolise cultures where people co-exist, but don't acknowledge each other's existence or right to participate in institutions as equals. <br />This could describe Christians and Jews, Christians and Muslims, whites and blacks, men and women, the rich and the poor. <br />This differentiation exists everywhere, Mieville's genius is to explore it through a highly abstract, but powerful, metaphor. <br />So comparisons with both Kafka and Chandler are apt.<br /><br /><b>Collins St, 5pm</b><br /><br />COLOUR SPOILER ALERT<br /><br />I don't want to spoil the visuals of the book for you, but below is a link to a wiki article about a 1955 painting that the book evokes for me.<br />The painting explores a similar concept as a metaphor for alienation.<br />One warning before you view it though: Partly because of the cover, I imagined The City as a blue/grey world, the world of this painting is brown.<br />So if you want to leave scope to choose your own colours, you might not want to visit the link until you've read the book.<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Collins_St.,_5_pm">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Collins_...</a><br /> <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9525343382234155965" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3073602842" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3073602842" />
    <a title="Leonard Gaya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27098959-leonard-gaya"><img alt="Leonard Gaya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442984307p2/27098959.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3073602842?book_show_action=true">Oct 18, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leonard Gaya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leonard Gaya" href="/user/show/27098959-leonard-gaya">Leonard Gaya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11143207-the-city-the-city">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3073602842" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12203616954565410593">China Mi√©ville is probably one of the most acclaimed British sci-fi and fantasy authors today (perhaps in the overcasting shadow of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1221698.Neil_Gaiman' title='Neil Gaiman' rel='nofollow noopener'>Neil Gaiman</a> and a couple of others, but still). <i>The City & the City</i> does have weird speculative elements but isn‚Äôt a science fiction novel proper. It is nonetheless an incredibly clever piece of fiction, at the junction of different genres. In a way, it works like the city the book talks about: in short, this is <i>The Book & the Book</i>.<br/><br/>On the one hand, <i>the Book</i> (setting</span>
  <span id="freeText12203616954565410593" style="display:none">China Mi√©ville is probably one of the most acclaimed British sci-fi and fantasy authors today (perhaps in the overcasting shadow of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1221698.Neil_Gaiman" title="Neil Gaiman" rel="nofollow noopener">Neil Gaiman</a> and a couple of others, but still). <i>The City &amp; the City</i> does have weird speculative elements but isn‚Äôt a science fiction novel proper. It is nonetheless an incredibly clever piece of fiction, at the junction of different genres. In a way, it works like the city the book talks about: in short, this is <i>The Book &amp; the Book</i>.<br /><br />On the one hand, <i>the Book</i> (setting-wise) is set in a city that seems to come straight out of some of Calvino‚Äôs <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9809.Invisible_Cities" title="Invisible Cities by Italo Calvino" rel="nofollow noopener">Invisible Cities</a>. The urbanisation depicted in the story is imaginary, possibly situated somewhere in the Balkans or around the Black Sea. What is quite fascinating is that it is not one city, but two completely separate cities, possibly even rival cities, that are sharing the same geographical space. This might suggest something like Berlin before the fall of the Wall, or perhaps the disputed status of Jerusalem in the Israel-Palestine conflict. But in Mi√©ville‚Äôs fiction, the cities of Besel and Ul Qoma share the same streets, the same buildings, and yet work like completely foreign entities. A schizophrenic place, where people from one city are trained to shun whatever or whoever belongs to the other city:<br /><br /><blockquote>Ul Qoman man and Bes maid, meeting in the middle of Copula Hall, returning to their homes to realise that they live, grosstopically, next door to each other, spending their lives faithful and alone, rising at the same time, walking crosshatched streets close like a couple, each in their own city, never breaching, never quite touching, never speaking a word across the border. (p. 133)</blockquote><br /><br />Such an insane situation is almost at the level of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/40961427.1984" title="1984 by George Orwell" rel="nofollow noopener">1984</a>. Added to this bipolar city, to this doppelg√§nger urbs, is Orciny, a third dimension, into the past, the archaeology of both cities, the Ur-urbs, that once was one.<br /><br />In a nutshell, <i>The City &amp; the City</i> is an utterly compelling novel on the subject of borders: borders between countries, spaces, economies, religions, languages, people, borders even inside one‚Äôs brain ‚Äî and the fact that these boundaries are at the same time tangible (‚Äúbreaching‚Äù through the frontier between cities has dire consequences for the trespassers) and entirely imaginary, a mental construct. One can hardly overstate how relevant this is to our current reality, where open globalisation coexists with communitarianism and segregations of all kind, to the point where people purposefully cease even to see (unsee) their neighbours and fellow humans and consider them foreign, barbarian and hostile.<br /><br />But then, on the other hand, <i>the Book</i> (plot-wise) is also a classic murder mystery/police procedural ‚Äî with evidence gathering, search warrants, interrogations, etc. ‚Äî, in the tradition of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/16927.Dashiell_Hammett" title="Dashiell Hammett" rel="nofollow noopener">Dashiell Hammett</a> or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1377.Raymond_Chandler" title="Raymond Chandler" rel="nofollow noopener">Raymond Chandler</a> (note the similarities in the names of the detectives, for instance: Marlow / Borl√∫), spiced up with a dash of conspiracy theory and secret societies, redolent of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1730.Umberto_Eco" title="Umberto Eco" rel="nofollow noopener">Umberto Eco</a> or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/630.Dan_Brown" title="Dan Brown" rel="nofollow noopener">Dan Brown</a>. Somehow, this sort of genre ‚Äúcrosshatching‚Äù reminded me also of J.G. Ballard‚Äôs early novels, for instance, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16234584.The_Drowned_World" title="The Drowned World by J.G. Ballard" rel="nofollow noopener">The Drowned World</a>, where Ballard combines a fascinating setting (London as a prehistoric laguna) with a rather conventional adventure story.<br /><br />And so, although Mi√©ville manages to play his cards and weave his double-city concept quite decently into the police crime genre, the whole investigation between the cities tends to muddle the stakes of his initial idea into something that feels a bit formulaic and chewy. By contrast, in the realm of whodunits + speculative dystopia, <i>Blade Runner</i> was a masterful example, mainly because it ultimately made us <i>feel</i> the metaphysical question of what it means to be mortal. Mi√©ville, in my view, falls somewhat short of creating a similar masterpiece, because he fails to push his idea on borders, identity and otherness quite to the same hypnotic degree. He remains, instead, in the rut of a murder investigation, with diminishing marginal results.<br /><br /><i>Edit:</i> Just finished watching the four-episodes BBC miniseries adaptation of the novel. It‚Äôs a decent piece, with a very interesting visual rendition of the city imagined by Mi√©ville. The plot and characters differ somewhat from the book however ‚Äî there is a whole thread about inspector Borl√∫‚Äôs wife which is completely made up by the screenwriters, possibly to enhance the audience‚Äôs emotional involvement. Still, the ending is just as confusing (if not more confusing!) as in the book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12203616954565410593" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer172905722" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer10187391296941424207">Ugh, I feel like such a jerk. This book has received such praise, so my expecations were pretty high. I had read more than my fair share of excellent reviews, so I felt I was in for a treat.<br/><br/>I really tried to like this - I really did. I thought the premise was absolutely brilliant. I just felt like it was either his prose or just the way the story itself came together that I didn't "get". I've yet to read a book that made me feel so confused.<br/><br/>Please don't hate me goodreaders! I tried, I really did</span>
  <span id="freeText10187391296941424207" style="display:none">Ugh, I feel like such a jerk. This book has received such praise, so my expecations were pretty high. I had read more than my fair share of excellent reviews, so I felt I was in for a treat.<br /><br />I really tried to like this - I really did. I thought the premise was absolutely brilliant. I just felt like it was either his prose or just the way the story itself came together that I didn't "get". I've yet to read a book that made me feel so confused.<br /><br />Please don't hate me goodreaders! I tried, I really did.</span>

<div id="review_170676993" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/170676993" />
    <a title="Apatt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt"><img alt="Apatt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349239948p2/2457095.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/170676993?book_show_action=true">May 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Apatt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Apatt" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt">Apatt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer170676993" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1073062888887441552">You don‚Äôt have to be crazy to read this book, but it helps.<br/><br/>Nah, I jest. The basic idea of this book is not hard to understand, but it is a springboard to an extraordinary level of weirdness. The book is set in two cities that occupy the same geographical space. Imagine two cities existing side by side and then whisk them so that they are all jumbled up. That is one way of looking at the setting. The citizens of Besel* and Ul Qoma are not permitted to interact with the citizens, objects, or groun</span>
  <span id="freeText1073062888887441552" style="display:none">You don‚Äôt have to be crazy to read this book, but it helps.<br><br>Nah, I jest. The basic idea of this book is not hard to understand, but it is a springboard to an extraordinary level of weirdness. The book is set in two cities that occupy the same geographical space. Imagine two cities existing side by side and then whisk them so that they are all jumbled up. That is one way of looking at the setting. The citizens of Besel* and Ul Qoma are not permitted to interact with the citizens, objects, or ground space of the other city. They are not even allowed to sense them except to avoid a collision. Of course actual seeing and hearing cannot be helped but they have to ‚Äúunsee‚Äù, ‚Äúunhear‚Äù, ‚Äúunsmell‚Äù etc., a kind of mandatory constant denial.<br><br>The main story is basically a murder mystery where a body of a ‚ÄúJane Doe‚Äù is found and it is the job of a police detective to find her killer. It turns out that both cities are involved and maybe even a third secret city also occupying the same location. The world of <i>The City &amp; The City</i> is actually Earth apparently in the present day with references to the internet, MS Windows and Tom Hanks movies to vaguely indicate the time period. The two city-states are located in what appears to be Eastern Europe.<br><br>Usually, I try to avoid reading a book‚Äôs synopsis before I start reading it. I enjoy discovering the plot, the setting and characters as I read, but as far as <i>The City &amp; The City</i> is concerned this may not be such a good idea, Mi√©ville does not start the narrative with any kind of exposition to set the scene. The readers are pretty much left to immediately fend for themselves from the first page. This is not really a problem if you already have a little prior knowledge of the very odd setting from a summary.<br><br>Reading this book is not a walk in the park, China Mi√©ville made an effort to create such a bizarre world and culture for you to explore and some exertion is expected from the reader. As can be expected of any China Mi√©ville book the writing is excellent, the characters are interesting (though take a little getting used to) and a little dry humour is scattered in several places. The climax is mind bendingly imaginative and the ending is rather poignant.<br><br><i>The City &amp; The City</i> defies simple categorization though ‚Äúspeculative fiction‚Äù is the most suitable as it is a very broad term that encompasses both science fiction and fantasy, and anything else that falls in between (maybe the fiction &amp; the fiction?). This review is of a reread and it made me realize that the major disadvantage of rereading a whodunit is that I already know who did it as soon as the character‚Äôs name is mentioned in the book, fortunately, the why of it, in this case, is fairly complicated and has to be rediscovered. More importantly <i>The City &amp; The City</i> has much more to offer than a conventional crime fiction novel. This is not a breezy read, but it is a book that I would like to recommend to everybody; give the ol‚Äô little grey cells a nice workout, and while it is challenging it is not overly formidable and certainly an enriching experience for the mind.<br><br>*The name of the Besel city seems to be spelled differently in some editions.<br>________________________________<br>Note:<br>I just realised that I have to do some "unseeing" all the time when I am typing on my English/Thai keyboard:<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1389690332i/8048373._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>When I am typing in one language I have to "unsee" the alphabets of the other language. It is very disorienting when I switch from typing in one language to another. Living in either Besel or Ul Qoma would quickly drive me insane.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1073062888887441552" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1190715310" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1190715310" />
    <a title="James Thane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3483907-james-thane"><img alt="James Thane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442513566p2/3483907.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1190715310?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James Thane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James Thane" href="/user/show/3483907-james-thane">James Thane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3483907-james-thane?shelf=crime-fiction">crime-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1190715310" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer974342635890782761">In <i>The City & the City</i>, China Mieville blends fantasy, sci-fi and crime fiction into one of the most interesting books I've read in a while. It's a tale of two cities set in eastern Europe. One, Beszel, is in decay; the other, Ul Qoman, is much more prosperous. The kicker is that the two cities share the same physical space and the citizens of one city are strictly forbidden from interacting with citizens of the other. <br/><br/>Citizens of one city are prohibited from even looking at each other or into e</span>
  <span id="freeText974342635890782761" style="display:none">In <i>The City &amp; the City</i>, China Mieville blends fantasy, sci-fi and crime fiction into one of the most interesting books I've read in a while. It's a tale of two cities set in eastern Europe. One, Beszel, is in decay; the other, Ul Qoman, is much more prosperous. The kicker is that the two cities share the same physical space and the citizens of one city are strictly forbidden from interacting with citizens of the other. <br /><br />Citizens of one city are prohibited from even looking at each other or into each others' cities. Should they do so accidentally, they are required to immediately look away and "unsee" what they have just seen. There is one legal border crossing where, with a good enough reason, a citizen of one city may legally cross into the other. To do so otherwise, or to notice something or someone in the other city without looking away, is to be in Breach, and the punishments for being in Breach are beyond severe.<br /><br />As the story opens, a woman is found murdered in Beszel, and Inspector Tyador Borlu of the Extreme Crime Squad is assigned to investigate. Borlu quickly discovers that the woman was actually killed in Ul Quoma and dumped in Beszel, which would be a clear case of Breach. Borlu assumes that the case will be taken away from him and that the killer or killers will be found and punished by the Breach authorities who deal with such matters.<br /><br />Because of a technicality, though, it turns out that the murder was not in Breach and so the case still belongs to Borlu. To investigate, he must cross over into Ul Quoma, where he is teamed with a detective named Qussim Dhatt who leads the investigation there. The two quickly discover that something much more sinister than a run-of-the-mill murder may be going on here.<br /><br />Tyador Borlu is, at heart, the archetypal detective with roots all the way back at least as far as Sam Spade and Philip Marlowe. He's determined to solve this case and find justice for the victim, no matter the odds and personal danger that might be involved. But he must do so in a world that neither Sam Spade, Philip Marlowe, Harry Bosch, nor any other such detective could ever have imagined.<br /><br />It took me a while to get into this book and to buy into the principle of the two cities, but once I did, I was completely hooked and couldn't wait for the climax. There's an awful lot going on in this story and I'm probably not smart enough to appreciate half of what Mieville is attempting to accomplish here. <br /><br />At one level, the book may be seen an allegory about the worlds that each of us live in and the worlds that occupy the same space but that we might prefer not to see. Think, for example, of the reaction that many people have when seeing a homeless person shuffling down the street, pushing his or her liberated grocery cart full of possessions along the sidewalk. The tendency we often have is to look away, or to "unsee" the person, and to proceed about our business as if problems like homelessness didn't exist in our world. <br /><br />This is a very interesting and stimulating book, one that the reader will be left thinking about for a long time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="974342635890782761" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2555441041" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2555441041" />
    <a title="Paul S√°nchez Keighley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25180638-paul-s-nchez-keighley"><img alt="Paul S√°nchez Keighley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1557221483p2/25180638.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2555441041?book_show_action=true">Oct 15, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul S√°nchez Keighley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul S√°nchez Keighley" href="/user/show/25180638-paul-s-nchez-keighley">Paul S√°nchez Keighley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330534192" href="/book/show/9791714-the-city-the-city">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25180638-paul-s-nchez-keighley?shelf=c-united-kingdom">c-united-kingdom</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25180638-paul-s-nchez-keighley?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25180638-paul-s-nchez-keighley?shelf=whodunit">whodunit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2555441041" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5984465033576053253">There was a time I hung out with Sudanese asylum seekers in Tel Aviv. Their status as refugees was never officialised, so they lived in a sort of legal limbo. Whenever we‚Äôd go for a walk, even if we were ambling down familiar streets, it felt like we were in a different city altogether. We‚Äôd turn into alleys I‚Äôd never noticed and find Eritrean families huddled around coffee pots on small fires; walk into previously unseen shebeens that stood quite literally between shops I‚Äôd frequent on a regula</span>
  <span id="freeText5984465033576053253" style="display:none">There was a time I hung out with Sudanese asylum seekers in Tel Aviv. Their status as refugees was never officialised, so they lived in a sort of legal limbo. Whenever we‚Äôd go for a walk, even if we were ambling down familiar streets, it felt like we were in a different city altogether. We‚Äôd turn into alleys I‚Äôd never noticed and find Eritrean families huddled around coffee pots on small fires; walk into previously unseen shebeens that stood quite literally between shops I‚Äôd frequent on a regular basis; rattle open a garage door to reveal a mass being held complete with clappy East African hymns... <br /><br />This book reminded of many such instances. In it, two cities, Bes≈∫el and Ul Qoma, share the same geography but never overlap, their turfs winding around each other like the pieces of an elaborate jigsaw, and whose citizens make a conscious effort to ‚Äòunsee‚Äô any people or buildings they cross on the street who are, in fact, in the other city.<br /> <br />The concept Mi√©ville explores rings so true it practically reads like a work of realism. It‚Äôs so easy to believe humans would be able to inhabit and willingly comply with the norms of such a stifling setting. This man is devilishly good at coming up with a screwball scenario and then exhaustively explore every possible implication it would have for its people, laws, philosophy, economy‚Ä¶<br /><br />It‚Äôs obvious enough Mi√©ville loves cities, but he also deeply understands their makeup. I loved the fact that Bes≈∫el has a little UlQomatown, and that there are underground anti-establishment movements made up mostly of young impressionable punks and old stoned hippies. These are the sort of details that make Mi√©ville‚Äôs cities feel alive.<br /><br />Perhaps my only complaint is that I wish the more existential aspects of the cities, which is touched upon in the first third, played a greater role in the story. As it progresses, the cities seem to become more of an interesting backdrop to a pretty straightforward murder mystery, and I found my interest waning when the book focused more on the crime than on the cities.<br /><br />Still, it‚Äôs a blast to read and I imagine I‚Äôll remember it often whenever I catch myself ‚Äòunseeing‚Äô certain parts or peoples of the city I live in.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5984465033576053253" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_986995917" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/986995917" />
    <a title="Ms. Smartarse" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13703358-ms-smartarse"><img alt="Ms. Smartarse" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1401273675p2/13703358.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/986995917?book_show_action=true">Oct 04, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ms. Smartarse" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ms. Smartarse" href="/user/show/13703358-ms-smartarse">Ms. Smartarse</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13703358-ms-smartarse?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13703358-ms-smartarse?shelf=murder">murder</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13703358-ms-smartarse?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13703358-ms-smartarse?shelf=urban-fantasy">urban-fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer986995917" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15946367818650533675">The victim of a gruesome murder has been found on the outskirts of the city of Beszel. A random woman dumped in a trash heap, doesn't appear to be more than the unfortunate victim of a sex-trade gone bad. However, when inspector Tyador Borl√∫ receives an anonymous tip, pointing fingers at the neighboring city of Ul Quoma, things start to get awfully messy.<br/><br/>You see, while jurisdiction between most neighboring cities may simply lead to petty police squabbles, Beszel and Ul Quoma actually make up two</span>
  <span id="freeText15946367818650533675" style="display:none">The victim of a gruesome murder has been found on the outskirts of the city of Beszel. A random woman dumped in a trash heap, doesn't appear to be more than the unfortunate victim of a sex-trade gone bad. However, when inspector Tyador Borl√∫ receives an anonymous tip, pointing fingers at the neighboring city of Ul Quoma, things start to get awfully messy.<br><br>You see, while jurisdiction between most neighboring cities may simply lead to petty police squabbles, Beszel and Ul Quoma actually make up two separate countries, albeit with multiple intersection points, but only one single border crossing. So you'd best "<em>unsee</em>" the <em>other</em> city in general, or else...<br><br><a href="http://pr2014.aaschool.ac.uk/submission/uploaded_files/DIP-02/Simon.Rowe-2.jpg" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1509305152i/24308334._SX540_.jpg" alt="Map of Beszel and Ul Quoma" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><em>Click for full size (beware of spoilers!)</em><br><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=China%20Mieville" title="China Mieville" rel="nofollow noopener">China Mieville</a>'s narrative voice just doesn't agree with me. I get bored of it in minutes, and my mind inevitably ends up wandering off. Initially I figured it was all me, whose English is not as fluent as I like to pretend.<br><br>I tried re-reading sentences several times, and even reading aloud. Unfortunately, most of it only led to my cat being put to sleep... more often, that is. I even tried to <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=73cqiJgZYhg" rel="nofollow noopener">listen to the author read an excerpt</a> during a book-related event, and it still didn't help.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1509305152i/24308335.jpg" alt="Sleeping cat" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><em>Caught mid-sleep, after 5 minutes of listening to me read aloud.</em><br><br>On the one hand, the prospect of dropping yet another book annoyed me to no end. On the other hand, this book has several glowing reviews from a lot of popular and accomplished reviewers, so I definitely had to be missing something. All I can say, is that somewhere along the 30% mark I found its appeal and couldn't let it go, till the story was finished.<br><br>Although the novel starts off as a police procedural, it eventually turns into a social commentary on the fictional life as a citizen in either of the two cities. <br>How do the inhabitants deal with having to ignore half of the things they actually see?<br>Why do these cities exist in this way?<br>How do foreigners adapt to these rules?<br><br><strong>Score: 3.8/5 stars</strong><br><br>Perhaps I should've just relented and read the Romanian translation (<strong>*BLASPHEMY!*</strong>), instead of getting annoyed at my lack of immersion. But we all know by now that I'm too proud for that. So I guess you'll just have to take my word, that if you persevere through the first 100 pages, there's a <del>high</del> chance that you'll get used to writing style, and start enjoying the rest of the book. <br><br>This will definitely <em>not</em> be my last Mieville book, but I'm already dreading having to get used to the writing style in his next book. &gt;_&lt;</span>
  <a data-text-id="15946367818650533675" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1343115190" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1343115190" />
    <a title="Gabrielle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6753400-gabrielle"><img alt="Gabrielle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598976963p2/6753400.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1343115190?book_show_action=true">Jul 23, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gabrielle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gabrielle" href="/user/show/6753400-gabrielle">Gabrielle</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330534192" href="/book/show/9791714-the-city-the-city">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=recommended-for-jason">recommended-for-jason</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=own-a-copy">own-a-copy</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=weird">weird</a>,
